Monday, 27 March 2006.

The Committee met at twenty-five minutes before four of the clock.

[The Deputy Chairman of Committees (LORD HASKEL) in the Chair.]

BBC Charter Review (Select Committee Report)

The Deputy Chairman of Committees (Lord Haskel): It may be helpful if I remind noble Lords that they speak standing. If a Division is called, the Committee will adjourn as soon as the Division Bells are rung and will resume after 10 minutes.

Lord Fowler rose to move, That the Grand Committee do report to the House that it has considered the second report of the Select Committee on the BBC Charter Review (HL Paper 128).

The noble Lord said: This is our second report on the BBC charter review in which, as our title rather inelegantly expresses, we examine further issues concerning the corporation. A word on the venue this afternoon: the choice was between having the Moses Room now or the Floor of the House at some indeterminate time in the future, so we decided it was best to have this debate now.

Among other issues, we looked at the licence fee, spectrum charging, the World Service, broadcasting of sports and religious broadcasting. I shall not try to deal with every one of those issues, but let me point to one unexpected success. In a committee which included the right reverend Prelate the Bishop of Manchester and the noble Lord, Lord Peston, who came to the issue of religious broadcasting from slightly different perspectives, we managed, surprisingly, to get an agreement. I intend to quit while I am ahead.

I thank the members of the committee for their work. I was talking to a journalist just before this debate who spoke of the real strength of the team, and I agree wholeheartedly. I also thank our staff—Richard Collins, our special adviser, Peter Hill-Jones, our excellent secretary, Rita Logan, and, above all, our Clerk, Chloe Mawson, who sits here today. It is always a good idea to keep well in with the adviser to the referee in case I go one or two minutes into extra time. That is probably my last reference to sport, not because it is not important but because I live in hope that the noble Lord, Lord Maxton, who is a great expert—certainly a greater expert than I am—will deal with it in his speech.

Our committee is beginning to get quite well known. I am now receiving letters which, if not quite hate mail, leave me in no doubt that their writers do not agree with the committee—and that is not just letters from the Government. I acknowledge receipt of a letter from the Abolish the BBC campaign which believes
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC232
 
that the licence fee is money demanded with menaces for an unwanted product. I acknowledge another letter which says:

    "For a former Conservative MP to support an institution that one perceives as at least broadly soft left and perhaps either consciously or unconsciously has an agenda to keep the Tories out of government is simply appalling . . . I can only conclude in your support of the licence fee that you have some hidden career agenda".

Hidden from the writer and, I have to say, hidden from me.

More encouragingly, I received a letter last week, forwarded to me because it was addressed to Norman Fowler, 10 Downing Street, Westminster. Of course, I knew there was a vacancy, but I had not realised it was an open competition. If I was Prime Minister—that is an extremely good link, as they say in the trade—I would be very disappointed with the White Paper served up by the Secretary of State, which inadequately addresses some of the issues that the committee has raised. For all the words about consultation on the future of the BBC, there has been next to no movement from the Government since their original Green Paper. In spite of the criticism, the White Paper persists with its eccentric model for the governance of the corporation, continues to add costs to the licence fee and rejects the case for proper parliamentary scrutiny and better outside regulation. We should be clear of the consequences; as things stand, neither the charter nor the licence fee can be effectively challenged in Parliament.

The committee is strongly in support of the BBC and services such as the much admired World Service. We also support the principle of the licence fee itself. The BBC is a world leader, with programmes such as "Planet Earth" and news reporting that is both objective and of a very high standard. We pay tribute but, equally, we do not see it as our role to be uncritical cheerleaders. Our concerns are that the public should get the best possible value from the money that they pay, that the BBC should attain the highest possible standards in programming and news reporting and should not use its position unfairly against competition, and that it should be independent of government and seen to be independent.

Here we come to a fundamental point in both our reports. The White Paper does not lead to legislation which Parliament can examine. There will be no Bill and no scrutiny in Committee. The decisions have been taken; this is a deal between the Secretary of State and the chairman of the BBC, albeit financed by the public. The question is whether that way of making decisions is in the public interest, and we believe that it is not.

With the licence fee, the BBC wants a seven-year settlement. Its proposal is that each year for the next seven years the fee should be increased by RPI plus at least 2.3 per cent—so there will be assured increases for seven years at over double the estimated rate of inflation. The BBC estimates that by the end of the period the cash bill for the licence-fee payer will be £180. There is now no disagreement on that figure, I believe, nor that the income of the BBC will rise to
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC233
 
more than £4 billion a year. The ITV estimate is that licence-fee income will soon outstrip all television advertising revenue across all television channels on all platforms. Perhaps it can therefore be agreed that spending on this scale needs to be carefully justified.

We do not believe that RPI should be taken as a base line for licence-fee increases, because we think that an automatic link gives no incentive for efficiency gains. We also believe that the BBC should cost its bids more carefully. Its estimated cost for the move to Manchester has now been reduced by £200 million—one-third—since its original bid was submitted. Even more crucially, we do not believe that government should place costs on the licence-fee payer—the licence fee is a regressive charge—when such costs should be borne by general taxation. The outstanding example is helping the most vulnerable viewers with digital switchover. Free licences for the over-75s is a social cost taken up by general taxation, but when it comes to social help on switchover the cost will be borne by the licence-fee payer because according to the Government it is a broadcasting cost. There has never been any serious attempt to justify the inconsistency.

At the same time we await the decision on the spectrum tax. Currently the BBC is allocated spectrum-free. The fear is that the Treasury is now pressing Ofcom to charge the BBC for the use of spectrum. We would then have the prospect of the BBC paying licence-fee money raised from the public for programme-making to the Exchequer. The curious decision of the Office for National Statistics that the licence fee is now a tax would be underlined in practice. It would be being used as a tax.

Not everyone may agree with the committee on these issues, but I hope that we can all agree that they are important and deserve to be examined. But how is this to be done? For the licence fee, the only opportunity will be an order placed before Parliament. Parliament can accept it or reject it, but it cannot change it or properly scrutinise it. This may be an arrangement which suits the Government, but whether it is in the public interest is another matter. No one is asking for primary legislation every year, year after year, but there is no reason why we should have primary legislation to decide the basis of the seven-year settlement—particularly given that we are talking about over £4 billion of public money.

The same criticisms apply to the charter. There is a take note debate, but no scrutiny—not even an order. Yet not everyone agrees that the charter is beyond debate and is completely uncontroversial. In the new governance arrangements we will now have a trust and an executive board taking the place of the governors, who, just in case we should forget, have been partly responsible for the worldwide reputation that the BBC enjoys today. Not everyone believes that a trust with one outside chairman and an executive board with the prospect of another outside chairman is necessarily an improvement on the present position. The potential for confusion seems enormous, because the White Paper states:

    "The BBC Trust is not a 'Trust' in a legal sense",


 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC234
 

and:

    "The new Executive Board will include non-Executive members".

Defending the solution, the Secretary of State said in her Statement to Parliament:

    "That is a unique solution for a unique organisation in unique circumstances".—[Official Report, Commons, 14/3/06; col. 1312.]

I am not sure that the Secretary of State quite gets our point. No one disputes that it is unique. Indeed, that is the kernel of our criticism. It is a solution that is unknown to man. The real question is whether it is right; and the committee is not alone in its doubts about that.

More broadly, our view is that the public has an undoubted and incontestable right that its money is used to best effect. That is why we advocate greater access to the National Audit Office. We had some fascinating evidence on that last Wednesday from the noble Lord, Lord Birt, who spent 13 years as director-general and deputy director-general of the BBC. He was frank. He said that the BBC that he joined was a wasteful organisation and that he had laboured long to put that right. But in the period that money was being wasted, did the public know that? Of course they did not. There was no way that they could know. The BBC was not accountable then to the public, and in my view it is insufficiently accountable now. It is for that reason that the committee advocated greater access for the National Audit Office, including scrutiny of the licence-fee bid.

The Government's response to what they called this "untrammelled extension" of National Audit Office access was that it would risk editorial independence and in some way conflict with the principle that the main line of accountability should run directly to the licence-fee payer. But how can the BBC be accountable to the licence-fee payer if the licence-fee payer is not provided with the information? Why, if independence is challenged, does that arrangement work perfectly well with the World Service, which says that it has actually improved matters? Am I not right in thinking that the chairman of this undesirable body—the National Audit Office—has just been appointed by the Prime Minister to check ministerial standards? The Government's case is very weak.

The Government make a wider point that the public do not want either government or Parliament to have a wider role. They base that on some dubious research which they say showed that only 9 per cent of the public thought that the Government should be responsible for holding the BBC to account when things went wrong, and only 4 per cent thought that it should be Parliament. However, those findings are more than slightly compromised by the fact that half the respondents, some 48 per cent, had not the first idea who was responsible for ensuring that the BBC provided services of an acceptable standard, and even more that no one seemed to understand the difference between government and Parliament. The net result is that the Government have taken their 9 per cent vote as a vote of confidence. They have made no change and retained power for themselves. In my view, that position cannot last.
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC235
 

Lastly, I shall say a word about the BBC World Service. The World Service is greatly admired and, frankly, it is extraordinary that we have not developed it even more than we have. We have a world leader, but, as in so many areas in this country for some reason, we seem incapable of developing it to its full potential. The World Service is financed not by the licence fee, but by general taxation through the Foreign Office. Frankly, I am not sure that this sponsorship is an advantage. The noble Lord, Lord Birt, told us that in his day the Foreign Office was reluctant to see the World Service go online—doubtless believing that this new, modern development would not catch on.

Even worse, we have a position today where the World Service intends to launch an Arabic language television service. Potentially, that could be an important development. The World Service would like to run it as a 24-hour service, but has been told that there are only the resources for 12 hours. The extra cost would be £6 million, compared with the £80 million the commercial service BBC World has lost since 1999. This seems a curious set of priorities.

In another way, the World Service also brings us full circle. The Government's original Green Paper was entitled A strong BBC, independent of government. The whole authority of the World Service comes from its independence—it is not a means of government propaganda. That is why the committee is concerned that the Foreign Office is setting up a new public diplomacy board, chaired by a Foreign Office Minister, where the World Service will be required to attend as an observer. Public diplomacy is defined by the Government, following the report of the noble Lord, Lord Carter of Coles, as:

    "work aiming to inform and engage individuals and organisations overseas, in order to improve understanding of and influence for the United Kingdom in a manner consistent with governmental medium and long term goals".

We see very real dangers in taking the World Service down that road. We do not believe that the World Service should be seen as a tool of public diplomacy or governmental goals, and we do not believe that it is appropriate for a representative of the World Service to serve on a board to be chaired by a Minister. The World Service, as the BBC generally, must always be seen to be independent of government. We do not believe that is achieved by that proposed policy.

I need no extra time. In essence, our view is that the Government have missed an opportunity for underlining the independence of the BBC, for increasing its accountability, and for constructive scrutiny of proposals affecting the BBC by the only body that has the real authority to do so, which is Parliament. I hope this committee, or a committee like it, will have the opportunity of following progress as months go by. Perhaps, as a debate seems to be beginning on the functions of the Lords, it is timely also to say that, in this respect, the Lords can play an important role. Select Committees like the BBC Select Committee have independence, and can draw on experience that I believe can be put to the public good. I beg to move.
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC236
 

Moved, That the Grand Committee do report to the House that it has considered the second report of the Select Committee on the BBC Charter Review (HL Paper 128).—(Lord Fowler.)

3.54 pm

Lord Peston: In my contribution I start more or less where the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, ended—with the independence of the BBC. I think we all agree that this is vital, and should not be taken for granted. Indeed, since independence includes the right to be mistaken as well as the right to be right, the BBC will always be subject to attack. Having said that, I do not believe—and nor did the committee—that independence is incompatible with transparency and public scrutiny. The committee reiterates the view, which has just been enunciated by the noble Lord, that parliamentary scrutiny of the BBC and the whole decision-making process needs strengthening, and your Lordships' House must play a larger role in that on a permanent basis.

It is most disappointing that, although your Lordships' committee recommended—this is related to independence—that the Nolan principles on standards in public life should apply throughout the BBC, the White Paper appears implicitly to reject this.

I turn to the question of the licence fee as a tax. The Office for National Statistics has made this change for national accounts purposes. The licence fee now appears in the national accounts on the tax side. As I understand it, BBC current expenditure will now be reclassified as public expenditure and appear on the other side of the national accounts, leaving the overall fiscal position unaffected. The ONS states that it is simply a statistical matter which has no implication for the independence of the broadcaster. From what little I know about the Treasury's position, it takes the same view. We end up with a tax that it is not really a tax.

However, it is not obvious that either the ONS or the Treasury is right. We have to ask, particularly since this expression is still used, whether it is still a "fee" at the same time. The report asks whether it is perhaps not a fee but a hypothecated tax. Paying the fee/tax is supposed to be a necessary but not sufficient condition for being able to receive TV broadcasts more generally. If you put it that way, it looks like a fee. It looks a fee also when you realise that, as far as my limited understanding tells me, the money is paid to the BBC. It is not paid to the Treasury to hand on to the BBC.

We could do with a much better answer from the Government on whether the licence fee is a fee or a tax. This is vital for me wearing my old academic hat, because I can see marvellous exam questions of the sort, "When is a fee not a fee but a tax? Answer with special reference to the BBC". But there is a serious side to it. We have not received that answer from the Government. Since it is a highly technical matter, I would be perfectly happy to accept a Written Answer rather a long, read-out briefing from the Treasury.

I turn to the RPI link. There are three questions that we need to ask. First, what in broad terms do we want the BBC to achieve? Secondly—our esteemed
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC237
 
chairman brought out this point—are these ends achieved efficiently? Thirdly, given the answers to those two questions, what financing formula follows? What seems to happen is the reverse; namely, we start from the RPI-plus base line and see what then follows for the BBC and its efficiency. That seems to be entirely the wrong way round.

The problem becomes even worse, as the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, said in his introduction, when one sees that the BBC's task seems to involve targets that are not obviously to do with what one thinks public sector broadcasting is about, not least the whole business of targeted help with the digital switchover and those sorts of matters. Having just passed the age of 75, I find it bizarre that the free TV licence for the over-75s seems to come out of social security expenditure, but that help with the digital switchover does not. It makes no sense. One is almost tempted to say that no one thought any of this through.

The only case I can see being made for RPI-plus is that it is an administrative convenience. If you cannot answer any of the fundamental questions, you can say "RPI plus a bit more" and forget about it all. Perhaps that is all that it is about, because as soon as you ask what the logical basis for the BBC's funding is, you end up with all sorts of very difficult questions.

The other day, there was a letter—I think from someone at the BBC—which stated that an appropriate comparison for the licence fee would be personal disposable income. The implication seemed to be that as long as it did not rise relative to personal disposable income, it was okay. From the point of view of economics, that is plain nonsense. We do not ask whether the ratio of anything else to personal disposable income should be held constant—or, indeed, whether that ratio has any meaning whatever. It is beyond me why anyone should suggest that it is all okay as long as it does not rise as a fraction of personal disposable income.

I could easily make a case for the licence fee rising more than its ratio to personal disposable income. I rather like the BBC and, like a great many other members of the committee, I think it does a very good job. But, equally, if someone asked me to make the opposite case for it to rise more slowly, I could do that as well, because that is the business economists are in.

I think we deserve some argument as to why what is proposed for the BBC is justifiable. That is what I, and I think the committee and noble Lords more generally, want to know. I reiterate that I speak as a devotee of the BBC and as a listener to Radio 3. I think that Radio 3 is worth the whole of the licence fee. I am therefore getting all the rest for nothing. I may be a bit unusual in that.

I turn now to religion, an issue mentioned by the noble Lord, Lord Fowler. I speak as someone who believes that there is already enough religious broadcasting, if not too much. I regard the existence of
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC238
 
a religious broadcasting department as an anomaly, but I am delighted that its name is now "Religion and Ethics". It certainly seems to me that public broadcasting has an important role in setting moral and ethical standards and in exposing the ethical dilemmas that occur so frequently in real life, from which religion is not separate. Equally, the role that religion plays in so many of the world's problems requires objective and expert reporting in news programmes and elsewhere, so I have no difficulty with any of that. As a firm believer in all committees coming to unanimous conclusions, I sign on to what we say, even though, as you can tell, there is an implicit agenda here for me—namely, if I were writing this, it would be a little different. But I support what is in the report.

I turn now to the controversy about "Thought For The Day". I preferred it when it was called "Lift Up Your Hearts". It is a pity that a secular contribution and interpretation is ruled out of this programme but it is not something that I would die in the last ditch for. Indeed, given the illogicality of what is often said in the programme, the case for secularism is typically made overwhelmingly by default. This is exactly what happened when I was at school, where the only effect of the compulsory religious instruction was to make atheists of all of us.

Your Lordships' committee had a difficult job to do. Under the leadership of the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, it has produced a very good report and indicated to us the direction in which we ought to go. Those of us who have been around a long time know that, in the short term, all governments, no matter what their political persuasion, take no notice of what committees such as ours recommend. But one should never be depressed about that because the evidence is that in the longer term such committees are influential—especially as Ministers are often desperate for some good thinking and will turn to our committee. I am confident that the guidance we offer in our report will, in due course, be followed.

4.04 pm

Baroness Bonham-Carter of Yarnbury: I welcome the opportunity to debate this second report and to thank again the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, for his excellent work as chairman. The report tested his abilities and qualities as a chairman even further than the first did. As he mentioned, its remit involved the broadcasting of sport and religion. Both subjects elicited lively differences of opinion from those on the committee. However, he managed to bring together, under a unanimous report, those with and without religious beliefs, to rein in the enthusiasms of the champions of, variously, cricket, soccer and rugby—I know a lot more about Scottish rugby now than I ever thought I needed to—and to rather deftly rebuff those lobbying for bridge to be regarded as a sport.

Since the second report was published, the Government have finally produced their White Paper. As has been much referred to in the press, it extols the
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC239
 
BBC to "take fun seriously" and make entertainment "central to its mission". Speaking as a member of the committee's cricket lobby, I suggest some live test matches. By failing to bid when the English cricket board last issued an invitation for its broadcasting rights, the BBC is "partly responsible", as our report says, for the fact that the ECB then went on to enter into an exclusive deal with BSkyB. The result—no live cricket on any free-to-air channel for the next four years. The BBC's funding by the licence fee and its other public service broadcasting responsibilities mean that it should have bid.

As we all know, the publication of the White Paper is a significant political event. It is significant because of the unique role that the BBC plays in the life of the nation, because it comes after one of the most damaging episodes in the BBC's history—the Hutton inquiry—and because it comes when we are experiencing unprecedented change in the world of broadcasting. It is to be welcomed in its endorsement of a,

    "strong BBC, independent . . . and responsive to public wishes",

and in its support of the licence fee. However, in so many areas it does not address the concerns voiced in the report.

I think that most people have always felt the licence fee to be a bit of a bargain—reliable news, some "Ab Fab", some Dickens, soothing David Attenborough and a bit of Jeremy Paxman to stir things up. Then there is all that radio, as the noble Lord, Lord Peston, mentioned, and now BBC online as well. However, the public's faith in the corporation will be put under severe strain if, at the same time as channels and choices multiply, the cost of the licence fee rockets due to the Government's insistence that the BBC—in other words, the licence-fee payer—funds the Government's policy of switching to digital.

As the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, has already asked, why should the cost of switching from analogue to digital not be paid out of general taxation? The licence fee is already a regressive tax. This makes it more so. Paying for switchover through it will particularly hurt low-income households, which already face covering the costs of replacing TV sets and recorders, and possibly updating their aerials. It gives grist to the mill of the enemies of the BBC who cry, "Poll tax".

There is worse, as the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, has said, but I feel so strongly about it I must repeat it. The cost of help for the over-75s and the vulnerable is also to be carried by the BBC. The Secretary of State is quite categorical about that. Her view is that a targeted assistance programme of help with digital switchover is a broadcasting cost. It is not; it is a social cost, and it is not appropriate for the licence fee to be used to pay for a social cost. This is in effect a new tax collected through the disguise of the licence fee and, again, it inflates the cost for every licence-fee payer.

To add insult to injury, the Treasury is set to make a substantial amount of money out of the sale of the analogue spectrum.

Baroness Bonham-Carter of Yarnbury: On the matter of spectrum charging, clearly the BBC should not have to pay for digital spectrum. That would involve the licence fee going straight back to the Treasury, or what the noble Lord, Lord Puttnam, rather colourfully referred to in evidence to us as "double dipping". The multi-channel landscape of the digital future is one that poses particular challenges to all public service broadcasters. The BBC is the cornerstone but it must not become a monopoly supplier. It is vital that Channel 4 is not left to flounder for lack of finance—a point made by the noble Lord, Lord Birt, when he appeared before us last week. The Government must consider allocating free spectrum to Channel 4 as well.

Then there is the leading role that the BBC is being asked to play by the Government in an enterprise that may not be wholly popular. I agree with Jocelyn Hay, chair of the Voice of the Listener & Viewer, in fearing that, having been put in charge of switchover by politicians,

    "the corporation will increasingly be perceived by the public as a quasi-government agency".

That brings me to another area that we covered in the report where the interventionist style of this Government gives cause for concern—the World Service. The noble Lord, Lord Fowler, has already mentioned—so I shall not quote again—the review on public diplomacy commissioned by the Foreign Office and the Treasury, which has given us all cause for concern. It is essential that the World Service is, and is perceived by its listeners to be, editorially independent. It must not be seen as a mouthpiece for the British Government if it is to continue to play its vital global role as a source of impartial international news. This is particularly important in the world we live in today and as it embarks on launching its Arabic television service—an enterprise which is to be supported wholeheartedly.

I want to say one last word on the injunction that the BBC must "take fun seriously" and that entertainment—the new buzzword which seems to have leapfrogged information and education—should be,

    "engrained into its services wherever possible".

This must not result in subjects that are difficult to make entertaining being marginalised. On 10 March, The Guardian ran a story about BBC1 having sunk to its "lowest peak-time rating" on a night that "Panorama" was given a peak-time slot. "Panorama", said The Guardian, "failed to woo viewers". Of course the BBC must entertain but it also has to take on the difficult subjects, and not be damned if it does because these do not always attract large audiences. Factual and current affairs programmes must be protected.

4.22 pm

The Lord Bishop of Manchester: It must be a constant frustration for those who frequently serve on Select Committees to realise that not only will much that is recommended not be accepted by the government of the day, but that on some matters the government, from the start, will let it be known that they are immovable. Just so with this Select Committee. As the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, indicated, charting the journey from Green to White Paper demonstrates how sparse has been the acceptance of recommendations made in the first report. Suspicions about done deals between the BBC and government, without appropriate scrutiny, inevitably raise proper questions about whether such methodology best serves the public interest.

The second report has a somewhat different role from the first—not least because of its timing and much of its content. It may have come too late to have significant influence on government thinking, but that does not necessarily diminish its longer-term importance as an indicator of some of the issues that the BBC itself needs to take seriously in the years ahead if the licence-fee payer is to be deemed as having value for money, and as a way of drawing attention to the need for effective scrutiny of what the noble Lord, Lord Birt, tellingly described as a wasteful organisation.

Indeed, this report, with its wider brief, is an example of the value and role that would accrue from a permanent Select Committee, for which the noble Baroness, Lady Howe, has so eloquently argued, as we move into an era of unprecedented change in the culture of programme-making and broadcasting, irrevocably influenced by the huge technological advances that are now gaining unimaginable momentum and of which the noble Lord, Lord Maxton, has often powerfully reminded us. I very much hope the Government will see their way to establishing such a committee—I believe the time is right.

The proposed move by the BBC of some departments to what the report prefers to call Greater Manchester to avoid seeming to favour Salford or Manchester one above the other—thereby saving me, as the diocesan bishop for both, any embarrassment—has gained widespread endorsement from the Government, the Select Committee, the local
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC242
 
authorities and the North-West Development Agency, and what now seems to be the unswerving commitment at the highest levels of the BBC. I think the way the financial figures have been bandied about for the costs of the move has been extremely unhelpful. I recognise that until the licence-fee settlement is reached, the scope of what is affordable for the whole of the BBC's output remains unclear, but that is not helped by the Government beginning to use the licence fee as tax. But there was something ludicrous about the sudden drop in proposed costs by £200 million. One of the national newspapers quoted me as asking humorously if there was a similar drop still to come. Humorous it may have sounded, but it was meant as a wry comment on what had seemed at one point to be figures plucked out of the air. I hope that a properly costed move, that gives reasonable long-term value to the licence-fee payer, will soon be confirmed to one of the excellent locations in Greater Manchester. Incidentally, one of the concerns that I and other noble Lords have raised about help over digital switchover for the elderly and disabled licence-fee payer still awaits a satisfactory response.

Undoubtedly, one of the most exciting things about the new location will be the opportunities for the much-vaunted creative hub. The Government's emphasis on the need for creativity, especially the window, has been consistently welcomed by the Select Committee, but whether the hub will be able to turn vision into reality remains uncertain. Intriguing evidence was given to us from ITV by Charles Allen. His reputation as a quick-thinking, astute businessman and strategist is well known in Manchester, where his brilliant efforts in relation to the 2002 Commonwealth Games provided the benchmark to which Melbourne has had to aspire.

In his evidence to the committee, Mr Allen rightly and robustly questioned the financial projections the BBC had made about the move north. However, several people in evidence had told us of the reputation the BBC has for its inability to work in partnership with others. That in itself will require the director-general and the chairman, by then in transmogrified form, to take the utmost care to make sure that the way the BBC operates in the hub belies that reputation. They might be forgiven for wondering why, when such a reputation is alleged, ITV, through Charles Allen, should be so very keen to work with the BBC. It would be ungracious for me to suggest that either side should sup with each other with long spoons, but I think some canny awareness would not go amiss and is no doubt very present. It would, in any case, be inappropriate for ITV to benefit from licence-fee money, particularly when it has recently walked away from some of its public service obligations and could even, at some stage in the future, be owned from outside the United Kingdom.

The media hub is a very big idea, but there are some major questions and uncertainties yet to be resolved about its composition and workability—for example,

over independents, which, rightly, are also proposed for inclusion. How will a balance be achieved between American or other foreign-financed independents and small national or regional companies? What about the other publicly owned broadcaster? Will Channel 4 be able to participate in this?     "to find new, innovative and informative ways of tackling issues of religion, spirituality, ethics and values through all the different programming genres".

the appointment and the swiftness with which it is made, as well as by the outcome of the BBC's current review of the role of its Central Religious Advisory Committee, to which our report refers. Meanwhile, I express gratitude to the colleagues with whom I have sat on this Select Committee for our informative deliberations, rigorous questioning and the spirit of courtesy and tolerance that has so characterised our meetings.

Lord Maxton : I apologise for being a few minutes late. I can only blame Virgin Trains for that.

Before I move to the major theme of my speech, I want to add my support to those who have spoken in favour of the BBC being covered by an Act of Parliament and having proper parliamentary scrutiny. I understand the arguments about keeping the BBC free of political influence; however, no other organisation but Parliament—particularly the House of Commons—can represent the licence-fee payers. They are, after all, the electors as well as the licence-fee payers. No one but Members of Parliament can claim to be their representatives. There are organisations which try to do so—but they do not. Such organisations have few members and only the House of Commons can be the licence-fee payers' representative. In a democracy, public-funded bodies must be responsible to the people through their elected representatives.

Although I endorse all the conclusions of the report, I have not been as over-concerned as some of my colleagues with the governance issues in the Green and White Papers, and others are better qualified to speak
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC245
 
on those issues. I am more concerned with what the BBC will do for the licence-fee payer over the next 10 years and what it will provide in services. On the previous occasion, I looked at its role in the world of changing technology; this time I shall concentrate on one aspect of the report—namely, the broadcasting of sport. I am sure most of my colleagues will be delighted about that. I asked the committee to look at this issue as I believe it is an important part of broadcasting. Certainly one of the BBC's major competitors—Sky—uses its sports coverage as a major means of attracting new subscribers.

I begin by congratulating the BBC on its broad and in-depth coverage of the recent Commonwealth Games in Melbourne. The showing of a large number of sports which normally never get any TV coverage was very welcome. I also take the opportunity, on behalf of all Members, to congratulate the sportsmen and sportswomen from all the nations of the United Kingdom on their tremendous achievements in Melbourne. The Committee will not be surprised, of course, that I think Scotland did particularly well.

Unlike some of my colleagues, however, as a Scot, I am not gravely concerned about the failure of the BBC to show test match cricket. I believe, however, that showing sport—particularly sports that are not universally popular—is part of the public service remit of the BBC. Increasingly, medical research shows that a healthy lifestyle must include regular exercise if it is to be effective. Sport on television encourages a percentage of those watching to take exercise, as well as being a major source of entertainment. In particular, coverage of events such as the London Marathon and the Great North Run, which show ordinary people—even people older than myself—taking part in a televised event, encourages others to take up exercise. Encouraging even a small proportion of people to undertake physical exercise, which will be both good for their health and reduce the burden on the National Health Service, is something that the BBC and other broadcasters should do as part of their public service remit.

However, not all licence-fee payers want to watch sport. Indeed, only a minority watch sport on a regular basis. Thus the BBC is limited on its two terrestrial channels as to the amount of sport it can show. One of the reasons that it did not bid for test match cricket in this country is because a test match can take up to five whole days to complete, and the BBC did not believe that that would be fair on other licence payers.

In the new digital world—which will be almost universal by 2012—there are three ways in which the BBC will be able to encourage sport by increasing its range and output. First, the BBC must consider how it uses its present digital channels, such as BBC Three and BBC Four, for sports coverage as well as for entertainment. It should also consider using its UK Gold channels, in partnership with others, to show immediate replays, as it did during the Six Nations rugby competition. It should show the enormous archive of great sporting events that it holds. This is tremendously important. A great many people want
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC246
 
not only to watch for the umpteenth time England winning the World Cup in 1966, but all kinds of other events as well.

While I accept that the setting-up of a new BBC channel will be difficult to achieve, I believe that there is a very good case for the BBC having a separate sports channel, not only for the reasons I have already given but to show—live—less popular sports, such as badminton, which may not be great viewing but which have a large participation among the public.

I wish to make two more points. First, we are concerned about the way in which the BBC is going to move towards more local television and radio. In my view, the BBC has an opportunity here to extend its role in sports coverage. Once you get genuinely local television, with news coverage in quite small towns, why should not the local youth cup final between two teams, which have played throughout a long competition, be shown? There is no reason why that should not be the case.

Secondly, the BBC should use its website as a very important part of sports coverage over the next few years. At the moment, it can produce live sports commentary in voice but not yet in viewing. But that will come. Live streaming on to the internet will happen—I apologise to the Minister if I am being a little technical for him—and there will be an opportunity to show sporting events that would not otherwise get coverage on television. It will be feasible for every Premier League football match to be shown live on the internet. That can be done either through the BBC or by the clubs themselves but, preferably, I think it should be done through the BBC. More particularly, again, the BBC would be able to show sports that would not otherwise get the same coverage.

I accept that this subject has not always aroused the interest of noble Lords but I believe it is very important that the BBC should continue its role as a major provider of sports broadcasting—and, in that role, it will continue to fulfil its role as a public service broadcaster.

Speaking at this stage in the proceedings, one is bound to be slightly repetitive, but I want to address two aspects of our report. First, as other noble Lords have said, what a sorry mess the Treasury is making of the clarity that should exist on what the licence fee is for and what the British licence-fee payer gets for it—and it is the Treasury that is doing that, of course, as in this respect the DCMS is merely a cat's paw for the Treasury. In loading costs on to the BBC, the Treasury has constructed a sort of zero sum game, whereby all the benefit of digital switchover goes to the Treasury and all the costs go to the licence payer. Therefore the Treasury does not, as it should, pay the costs of switchover, which is greatly to the benefit of UK plc, including the necessary social costs that are implied; but the Treasury takes the substantial extra income from the sale of spectrum, including a proposal to charge the BBC for digital spectrum use. Here you have to marvel at the sheer nerve of the Treasury, although those who have been Treasury Ministers will not be as astonished as me. The licence-fee payer will therefore be faced with a spectacular hike in costs in what is now described as a tax. I fear that the Chancellor is in danger of doing to the BBC what he has done for company pensions—destroying a well understood system by excessive opportunism and greed.

The other subject to which I shall refer at perhaps greater length has just been mentioned by the noble Lord, Lord King. A noble purpose is set out in the BBC White Paper:

    "Bringing the world to the UK and the UK to the world".

That is well said and is a great purpose in this age of globalisation, when the world stands sorely in need of shared understanding and enlightenment.

    "Nation shall speak unto nation"—

indeed. But if the BBC is the crowning glory of British culture, the World Service—if Paul Scott and ITV will forgive me—is the jewel in the crown, precisely not in the imperialist manner of the original analogy. The BBC World Service is a model of cross-cultural respect and tolerance, and upholds democracy, transparency and human rights. It possesses many of the qualities, including reason, objectivity, honesty and fairness, which this country at its best would like to think it stands for in the modern world.
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC251
 

That depends on the Foreign and Commonwealth Office, on whose grant-in-aid the World Service depends. It depends on the FCO recognising precisely the value that the World Service represents to the United Kingdom. Most of the time, the FCO has got it right. It knows that the BBC World Service creates a context of respect and appreciation for this country and its values. But I was very alarmed, as are other noble Lords, at the report by the noble Lord, Lord Carter of Coles, on so-called public diplomacy, which looked to the World Service as a way of promoting not the United Kingdom, but as others have mentioned, medium-term government aims and describes them as a tool of public diplomacy.

I detest the term "public diplomacy". Diplomacy is like making sausages or inventing party policy—it is an activity that is traditionally practised at best in private. I suspect that in practice we need to call public diplomacy by its proper name—propaganda. More fundamentally, medium-term British Government aims are exactly what the World Service should not be about. Imagine, as we say in our report, if every hour, on the hour, the BBC World Service, in the interests of honesty and transparency, had to have one of its dulcet-toned announcers say, "What follows is designed to promote the medium-term aims of the British Government". How credible, and, therefore, how useful would the service be in its proper aim of promoting British values? The BBC World Service does not exist to give the British Government a propaganda platform.

Throughout the Cold War, the World Service was listened to, often surreptitiously and with some danger, in preference to the blatant propaganda of the "Voice of America", by people who were then subject to totalitarian tyranny because they thought that the World Service was the "real thing"—not a sort of Orwellian loudspeaker. Successive governments, including this one, must have the humility and realism to recognise that "Brand Britain", as we call it in these days of new Labour, is more durable and more important than the priorities of any particular administration. Yes, the BBC World Service can set a benign context for the Government's external policies, but no, it is not a launch pad for British Government initiatives, any more than it is a promotional platform for British companies or a showcase for admirable British NGOs such as Amnesty or Oxfam. The Government, the companies, the NGOs—each and every one of them can benefit indirectly from a strong, independent World Service. The moment that independence is compromised, we have killed the goose that lays the golden eggs, and that will represent a grievous loss to this country and the world alike.

I was very struck by the evidence of the noble Lord, Lord Birt, last week—I am glad to see him in his place today—when, as a former director-general, he said unambiguously that it was not the role of the BBC World Service to promote public diplomacy. 27 Mar 2006 : Column GC252
 
categorical assurance from the Minister not only of the editorial independence of the World Service—which we have had on previous occasions—but that the Government do not regard the BBC World Service as a way of furthering their foreign policy objectives?

Baroness Howe of Idlicote: I am sure we are all very grateful to the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, for having secured this debate—the second in three months—because it enables your Lordships to comment not only on the work of the Select Committee but on the Secretary of State's White Paper. The background knowledge and leadership he brought to the task has been quite invaluable.

There are three areas where agreement on the way forward is clear. First, there is wide agreement about the BBC's quite unique cultural and financial value to this country. The World Service has been mentioned many times as the jewel, and that it is. Abroad, the BBC is perceived as the international benchmark for accuracy and independence. At home, the vast majority of licence-fee payers trust—that fast-disappearing dictionary word—the BBC.

Secondly, most would also agree that with technical developments moving even faster than the speed of light, and with digital switchover imminent, a further review of the way ahead will be needed about half way through the 10-year period. Thirdly, for the whole of that 10-year period, at least, the licence fee remains the best system for financing the BBC.

However, as your Lordships have already heard, there are disagreements. There are two to which I attach importance. First, only an Act of Parliament, rather than a charter, will bring the BBC under proper democratic parliamentary—as opposed to purely governmental—scrutiny. Secondly, as both Select Committees agreed, the trust/executive board system of governance for the BBC proposed in the Green Paper will not secure a BBC that is sufficiently accountable to licence-fee payers. Nevertheless, a continuation of the charter and the trust/executive board are the routes chosen by the Government.

If our committees have, so far, failed to convince the Government of the need for more radical change, then the next five years, with huge technological and content challenges ahead, will be an excellent time to see if in practice the Government's preferred model is sufficiently robust to meet today's licence-fee payer's expectations for, as the Secretary of State said, far greater transparency and separation of powers.

Certainly, on the face of it, the White Paper's principles of good governance set out a pretty robust agenda, with emphasis on the trust's involvement with, and accountability to, the licence-fee payer. Rightly also stressed is the need to protect—indeed, to strengthen—the independence of the BBC. As my noble friend Lord Birt, who has been quoted in this debate, said in his evidence to the Select Committee last Thursday, even if the trust/executive board is not
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC253
 
the preferred model of governance and accountability, perhaps we can see it as a step in the right direction. But noble Lords will want to keep a watching brief.

There will be, it seems to me, one very clear test of the ability of the new structures to deliver sufficient transparency and accountability; that is the extent to which the process by which new members of the trust/executive boards are appointed will or will not be truly independent of government. As the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, has pointed out, there is an ominous silence on that point.

A second indicator will be the way in which complaints about the content of programmes—not currently subject to regulation by Ofcom—are dealt with. The BBC trust has already put in place a more independent process for the first internal stage of dealing with complaints. But the licence-fee payer may well continue to have concerns about the absence of a more robust and obviously independent regulator. Our first report's proposal, which the Government have rejected, was that Ofcom should take ultimate responsibility for all content complaints, including that of accuracy and impartiality, currently the responsibility of the BBC governors. However, because of the perceived economic predominance of Ofcom's work, we argued that the content board should become more open and transparent. At present, we know far more about the work of the Consumer Panel—even though we are told it is no more than an advisory body to Ofcom—because it regularly publishes its reports. If the content board sometimes met in public and, above all, regularly published its findings, making clear when its decisions had been reversed by the main Ofcom board, that would generate much more confidence in its independence and credibility.

The next few years will be a testing time for these structures. But, equally, all that reinforces the clear need for the establishment of a permanent communications Select Committee of your Lordships' House. The expertise undoubtedly exists in this House—I could not have been more convinced of that by sitting on the board—and it would also meet the growing concern that no effective parliamentary accountability exists for matters which are so blatantly of public interest.

Regarding the charter itself, like the noble Baroness, Lady Bonham-Carter, I was delighted that entertainment has been re-emphasised as a clear purpose; and that the promotion of education and learning remains.     "promotion of education and learning",

I turn to the contents of our second report. The subjects on which evidence was taken were definitely issues of current concern. For me, by far the most important and interesting subject on which we took evidence was the role of BBC and other public service broadcasters in covering religious issues. Our debates were all the more stimulating, as our Select Committee membership included those of religious beliefs and none. Perhaps I may say that on this occasion the Select Committee was particularly lucky to have the expertise of the right reverend Prelate the Bishop of Manchester to aid our deliberations.

There is, of course, little doubt that people's religious and other spiritual beliefs are relevant issues for the BBC. Indeed, the Communications Act 2003 states that such coverage is a specific duty, and rightly so. But as Mark Thompson made clear in an article in January's Tablet, this has been the most controversial subject of his tenure so far. It may be thought that that is unsurprising, given 9/11, Iraq and all that has followed; but that is no reason for running away from the subject—and we did not. Although the BBC already covers that ground with a range of programmes, our recommendations stressed the importance of using a wide definition of religion throughout the different genres. We stressed, too, that the BBC needs to search for ever more innovative and informative ways to tackle issues of religion, spirituality, ethics and values.

There was concern also about news programmes, as the right reverend Prelate the Bishop of Manchester pointed out. The lack of people with sufficient expert knowledge to be able to give audiences the background knowledge to understand the context of the story must be remedied. Living as we do in increasingly multi-cultural and multi-faith communities, helping to create an atmosphere of understanding, respect and
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC255
 
tolerance for each others' religious and spiritual views, is surely the most important contribution that the BBC and other public service broadcasters can make. Thankfully, we have seen recently some hopeful signs of collaboration on admitting a percentage of non-faith children to faith schools. If that can be combined with a determined effort by the leaders of each religion to make clear the central values and beliefs of each religion, and if we can all work harder to achieve understanding and harmony—as the Prince of Wales said in Cairo, that ancient heart of Muslim learning a few days ago—I hope that the power and influence of the extremists will begin to diminish at last. The public service obligations of the BBC should be right at the heart of that process.

We are all signed up as BBC groupies, but the BBC must be seen to perform within today's very relevant expectations of openness and independence.

5.18 pm

Lord Macdonald of Tradeston: I congratulate the Select Committee, in particular its chairman, the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, on the breadth, quality and insight of its reports. As a former broadcaster, I was tempted to opine on almost every issue covered, but today I shall touch only on the broadcasting of religion. I declare an interest as chairman of the Parliamentary Humanist Group, so perhaps I can expand on a few of the matters about which my noble friends Lord Peston and Lord Maxton, in the interests of unanimity, said a little less than perhaps they might have.

I started my broadcasting career over 40 years ago on a religious programme, "Sunday Break". Subsequently, in my work for almost 20 years with Granada in Manchester and for 13 years with Scottish Television in Glasgow, at times I had the benefit of having my own committee of religious advisers, as I was responsible for religious broadcasting. We had extremely good relations, despite the fact that my secular beliefs were clearly declared. We worked very well together and made a number of excellent series. One reason was that we had a shared interest in many areas of ethics, social matters and spirituality and so on. Then, and more so now, in television the squeeze on the time available for slots that cover issues of that kind was increasingly intense, and of course we were always the poor relations when it came to budgeting. So, in some ways, I want to make a common cause here.

I welcome very much what the noble Baroness, Lady Howe, said in her excellent contribution and I hope to build on some of her remarks. The Communications Act 2003 gives the BBC a duty to make programmes on "religion and other beliefs". In the debates on the Communications Bill, it was made clear that that included non-religious beliefs, such as humanist ones. It was also made clear by the Minister of the day, my noble friend Lord McIntosh.

However, that duty does not seem to be taken seriously by the BBC as it relates to programmes about non-religious beliefs, such as humanism. The British Humanist Association has entered into
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC256
 
correspondence with the BBC since the Communications Act received Royal Assent. The repeated response to the request that non-religious and religious beliefs be given equal time in broadcasting is that most of the BBC's programming is secular. That seems to me to miss the point entirely. It suggests that non-religious licence-fee payers who want programmes on their beliefs of an equivalent depth to programmes on religious beliefs should somehow be satisfied with secular game shows, dramas and soap operas.

The recommendation of the Select Committee that,

    "the BBC should review its programme output to ensure that it complies with the Communications Act 2003 by providing services of a suitable quality and range dealing with religion and other beliefs",

is welcome in that it urges the BBC to meet its obligations in relation to those "other beliefs". But the concern must be that the BBC will not be responsive. Dealing with it in the past gives good reason for pessimism.

On the question of the broadcasting of non-religious beliefs as part of a public service, people with humanist beliefs are not seeking any privileges. Correspondingly, we are concerned that those privileges are accorded to the adherents of religion simply by virtue of their beliefs. There are two major institutional examples of that kind of privilege being granted to religion. One is the education system and the other is the BBC.

Broadcasting offers by far the most important means for members of a modern, open society to communicate with each other and jointly address the challenges that face them. A large number of people in the United Kingdom are non-religious. A rather loaded question in the 2001 census put our number at over 15 per cent of the population—around 10 million. But other polls and surveys consistently put the number higher—at around 25 per cent of the population, especially among the young. These people are leading their lives according to non-religious principles, but I believe they are just as entitled to have programmes that speak to them as are the religious people. Does the BBC assume that none of those millions has any interest in exploring what it means to live a life without religion, to find non-religious answers to the so-called "ultimate questions" and to articulate a non-religious basis for morality? It would seem so from its failure to provide any platform for such exercises, even while it continues to give a great deal of time to religion in many matters.

The programmes that we seek would not be attacks on religion but reflections on the basis of a secular morality, on particular moral issues, on a secular spirituality and on living a non-religious life, drawing on the tradition of non-religious life-stances from ancient times down to the present day. I believe that such programmes would help the large number of people who do not hold religious beliefs to explore what they do believe and how those beliefs affect the way they lead their lives. They could be expected in a small way to yield a social dividend—to help social
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC257
 
cohesion and commitment and to combat the moral anomie, cynicism and selfishness that some commentators perceive in modern society.

There are now many non-Christian religions and a far greater disparity within Christianity than Lord Reith could ever have dreamed of, let alone a popular renunciation of religion as a guiding force that would have alarmed him severely. There are also increasing numbers of people who are not renouncing religion but are simply growing up with a totally non-religious world view. I speak as someone who was brought up as an atheist, although my free-thinking parents insisted that I spent a great deal of my childhood at Bible class, the Boys' Brigade and Church of Scotland lectures, none of which I regret, but I came back to an atheistic view of the world and have maintained that. The BBC needs to regain its balance and neutrality by extending its programming to those citizens' beliefs and to include secular life-stances such as humanism in its coverage.

I shall finish on the question of the Central Religious Advisory Committee, or CRAC. As I said earlier, I had at my disposal committees of religious advisers. In Manchester, as I recall, we had Anglican, non-conformist and Catholic advisers. When I went to Scotland, we had Church of Scotland, non-conformist and Catholic advisers and, indeed, people from the "Wee Free" Church in Scotland. But even the Wee Free man was very consensual in his approach. Noble Lords will find that the religion that comes out of CRAC and elsewhere is very much middle of the road. Perhaps noble Lords would argue that there are no problems with that, but they would find very few "holy rollers" and very little fire and brimstone emanating from the religious committees in which I was involved, and I imagine that the same will be true of CRAC.

In the light of the Human Rights Act, which makes discrimination between people on the grounds of their religious or non-religious beliefs illegal, and in the light of the Communications Act, which does not give the BBC an obligation relating to "religion" but to "religion and other beliefs", it is surely clear that the Central Religious Advisory Committee should have humanist representation on it. Speaking from my own experience, I would say that our presence might actually help to make the slots more attractive by broadening their appeal. Sadly, the BBC has not offered such representation, so it is a disappointment to me that the Select Committee's recommendations on CRAC do not engage with this issue.

5.27 pm

Lord Kalms: Like any actor or author who has just received a rejection slip, I want to share that experience with colleagues in the Committee. When I was appointed to this distinguished House, I had an illusion that it was somehow linked to my long experience on some limited subjects—one of them being corporate governance—so the chance to plough that furrow on the BBC Charter Review Select
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC258
 
Committee was a welcome opportunity. But, as we have now discovered, our efforts were very much in vain and that is why my blunt rejection slip arrived.

My noble friend Lord King has pre-empted some of my comments but he has generously allowed me certain scope. However, as I am speaking on a single issue, I should like to put some meat on the bones of my contention and refer in particular to the DCMS review of the BBC Royal Charter and the draft charter and framework agreement, which sets out the constitution of the proposed trust board and executive board.

It is an interesting paper. It flies in the face of all known experience in this field. It flies in the face of the recommendations of our BBC Charter Review Committee, and it flies in the face of virtually everyone we interviewed, including the former chairman of the BBC. Their views ranged from trenchant criticism to damning by faint praise. I think it was fair to say that the noble Lord, Lord Birt, whom we interviewed only this week, was slightly more enigmatic, and I mention that in fairness.

As noble Lords are aware, the management of the BBC is to be changed from a single unitary board into a dual structure, with a top tier named the BBC Trust. It is accepted in the document that the word "trust" is not necessarily the correct one but it is being used in a colloquial sense, whatever that means in this context. It hints that a better name could be used and I shall try to oblige during the course of my remarks.

The purpose of the trust is to direct and monitor the activities of the BBC executive board. The list of the trust's functions include: to approve; to assess proposals; to assess performance; to define performance; to issue licences; to discharge; to hold to account; to set the framework; to conduct investigations—that is mentioned twice—to commission value for money; and, to ensure. Those words seem to be given out of context, but they are unambivalent in authority. These are deep and intrusive responsibilities. The role allows little discretion for subsidiarity. So perhaps it would be better if we started now by redefining the name of the trust to something more appropriate to its role. The word "distrust" somehow comes to mind. In truth, the trust is a pure regulatory body, appointed by the Government, and it would be disingenuous to call it anything else. A regulator by any other name does not smell like a rose.

The trust sits over the BBC board. It appoints a chairman and, if one has doubts about the trust, they bear comparison to those about the complexity of the BBC board itself. The executive board will be appointed and will include, inter alia, four non-executive directors. That is a revolution—a first time—a completely novel way in which to run a large institution. It is a ground-breaking, breath-taking phenomenon: non-executives being appointed to an executive board. My research through the panorama of UK companies showed that a non-executive on an executive board is about as rare as finding an unleaking Thames water main. It is a contradiction in terms. I am a strong believer in non-executive directors, as they bring a fresh view and skill to the top
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC259
 
table, but that role is totally neutralised and absolutely redundant if they will inevitably have thrust upon them executive involvement and, by implication, responsibility. It is an impossible scenario and should be so recognised.

I turn to the role of the board. The chairman and the executives, plus their sad passengers, have to submit to the trust or regulatory board virtually every detail of their management—their strategy and their operation. So to allow the trust to be in a position of judgment, the trust itself is recruiting is own substantial bureaucracy; when I first inquired, several months ago, it had reached 40 senior personnel, and was still growing like Topsy. Their function will be to ensure that nothing escapes their eagle eye as to the budget, the performance criteria and the guidelines covered under the headline words that I mentioned previously. Virtually nothing is outside their remit.

We have this extraordinary scenario in which the BBC executive and a large panoply of planners and budgeteers is fighting tooth and nail with another group of bureaucrats above them—the one below to get as much as possible, the one above to keep as tight a rein as possible. A great new corporate game is being established within the same organisation. The cost, the time, the delay, the process are the original ingredients of nightmares. The dynamics of the creativity of the BBC are becoming stuck in the morass of procedure. It is an extraordinary, useless waste of effort.

It is worth reflecting why this Frankenstein was created. Apparently, it was felt that the BBC was not independent enough of its own organisation—and the recent Kelly case seemed to indicate a prima facie case. The remedy would have been simplicity itself: an experienced chairman of any large company would have found a simplistic but robust procedure within the organisation to deal with what was clearly a defective process. A repair job was needed, not major surgery. But unfortunately the issue was a godsend to a bureaucratic mindset within the DCMS, and the BBC has had imposed upon it a suffocating regulatory straitjacket. I believe that the Secretary of State must be responsible, once again, for not seeing through the implications of her proposals.

The first question is: will it work? Of course it will work. Things work. Institutions work. It will be dramatically more expensive but that will be just another cost added. It will be monstrously inefficient, but that is also just another cost added. It will be a poisonous atmosphere, but that will affect only the players. Will it affect the quality of the BBC? I believe that that is inevitable.

Equally disturbing is that it sets an example of political incompetence, governmental interference and ministerial autocracy over a great British institution, which I hoped were no longer on the agenda—at least, I did before I was privileged to join the Select Committee.

I conclude on a happier note. As a connoisseur of chairmen and chairmanship, I compliment my noble friend Lord Fowler on leading the committee in a
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC260
 
skilful and incisive way. He is established in the upper quartile of chairmen and I am delighted, as an old dog, to have learnt new skills under his leadership.

Lord Barnett: I also congratulate the noble Lord, Lord Fowler, and the committee. I congratulate the noble Lord in one respect in particular. I know that he will forgive me for saying that because we are very good friends. I say that it is all a waffle for one simple reason, although there are a number.

First, we are told that there is a triple-lock system, the first of which is that the trust will issue licences. My experience of the board of governors and the board of management is that effectively they were already doing that job. I speak from experience, in particular, of the time when the noble Lord, Lord Hussey, was chairman and I was vice-chairman. Unfortunately the noble Lord, Lord Hussey, is too ill to be here today; I am sure we all regret that. In my experience, the board of management did its job, as I assume the executive under this so-called triple-lock system will do, and the board of governors did the job of the trustees. So there is no need whatever for this change.

Secondly, we are told that the BBC will have to have distinctive characteristics, such as being original, of high quality, challenging or innovative. It always did have those characteristics. There is nothing new in this triple-lock system. Thirdly, there will be a "public value test". That is a meaningless phrase and we all know that it is.

We are told on page 9 of the White Paper that there will be six public purposes for the BBC: sustaining citizenship and civil society; promoting education and learning; stimulating creativity and cultural excellence; reflecting the UK's nations, regions and communities; and bringing the world to the UK and the UK to the world. The BBC always did all those things and did them very well. The only one of the six points that it did not do is "Building Digital Britain", and I agree wholeheartedly with the committee that it
 
27 Mar 2006 : Column GC261
 
is not a matter for the BBC to build a digital Britain—it is a matter for the taxpayer to decide how it should build a digital Britain. The committee was absolutely right in that regard.

When I said that it is something of a waffle, I think that I underestimated—as my noble friend Lord Davies would say I always do—that each programme should have at least one of the characteristics set out on page 11, and that it should have it in mind to achieve every one of them. What about repeats? Will the repeats have to achieve that, and how often will they have to repeat them? I must say that, having often missed some of the programmes the first time, some of the repeats—especially the comedy programmes—I enjoy very much indeed; but they are hardly new. The fact that I enjoy them is not really important.

The other point, made in paragraph 3.1.7 in the report, is that the BBC should not always chase ratings. The Select Committee got it right when it referred to the fact that the BBC should not be confined to "worthy" programmes. Of course it should not. If they pursued their programmes in that way, the net result would be that they would lose audience figures and there would be a huge clamour that the BBC should be cut down to the size of their audience. So it is to create a vicious circle to suggest that they should not chase ratings.

We are also told, in paragraph 3.2.2, that "accuracy and impartiality" are important. I am not sure how the average BBC viewer and listener is able to decide what is accurate and impartial, given that the vast majority of our fellow citizens mainly read the tabloids—if read is the right word. So I am not sure how they will know, in the same way, of course, as we in Parliament obviously know, what is accurate and impartial. I know when I listen to the "Today" programme whether it is accurate and impartial because I know exactly what they are talking about.

The big issue is the significant issue of the independence of the BBC. To me that is vital. I raise the issue of the Office for National Statistics saying the licence fee was a tax, as I did when the Statement was repeated on 14 March by my noble friend. I hope my noble friend will forgive me if I say that his reply, in col. 41 of Hansard, was not a waffle—it was something worse than that. Certainly, it was not a reply to the issue. If the licence fee is a tax, all the expenditure is public expenditure. Perhaps all my years in the Treasury were a waste of time, but I put down some Written Questions to get a more realistic reply to the issue, and I got some replies, from my noble friend Lord McKenzie of Luton. It is worth reading one of them, at least. He said:

    "The process by which the BBC licence fee is raised and the proceeds passed on to the BBC is unaffected by the ONS decision".

Well, that is a straight statement. But then it goes on to say:

    "The decision does mean that, for the purposes of national accounts and Treasury spending presentations, the current expenditure of the BBC is added to measures of public spending
     

27 Mar 2006 : Column GC262
 
    as part of annually managed expenditure. BBC capital expenditure has, in any case, historically been included in measures of public expenditure".—[Official Report, 21/3/06; col. WA28.]

It is conceded that it is current expenditure, so who decided that it is not a matter for the National Audit Office and the Public Accounts Committee? I would not have thought it was a matter for the Treasury. It is a matter for Parliament, for the House of Commons, to decide on that.

So what does that mean? I suppose it is asking a lot of my noble friend to reply on behalf of the Treasury as well. I ask him to send me a letter and publish it in the Library some time or other to try and get some clarity about exactly what the Government mean if they agree with the ONS that it is public expenditure. No doubt I will get a reply at some point, and I look forward to it with great pleasure.

The real question is about the independence of the BBC, about which I care desperately. It is a tricky issue. If we are going to have some degree of parliamentary control—with which I agree as a parliamentarian, but am worried about as a former vice-chairman of the BBC—how on earth do we get that kind of independence? I am not convinced we should have it on a statutory basis instead of a Royal Charter. I am not at all certain that that is a good way of achieving true independence for the BBC. Indeed, if one did that, there would be a danger of risking parliamentary interference on a day-to-day basis. I am very worried about that.

I cannot say I have an actual answer, but, when a new charter is going to be set up for 10 years, the Committee is right to say that Parliament must have a say in how that kind of money is spent. The BBC's estimate of how it will spend money in Manchester is far from clear. Indeed, I fear that many senior executives at the BBC are unhappy about going to Manchester, and are delaying it and putting out ridiculous cost figures. If I may say so, they are quite wrong. Manchester is a booming city, and would be a wonderful place for BBC management to go to.

I am unhappy about the White Paper generally. I am unhappy about the incoherent replies—if I may put it so moderately—we have had so far. I hope that in its charter renewal the BBC will be sensible in its demands on costs.
